=== Removed Clozes with Index > 9 (Markup Only) ===

Note ID: 1585653262766
  Field: Text
    Before:
      The {{c2::memory}} is one of the main components of the Von Neumann architecture; it
stores {{c3::data}} as well as the {{c4::program}} to be {{c5::executed}}. In an {{c6::imperative language}}, the part
of the {{c2::memory}} used to store data is {{c7::abstracted}} by {{c1::variables}}, which represent {{c2::memory}} {{c11::cells}}. {{c6::Imperative languages}} provide {{c9::assignment}} statements to {{c10::modify their contents}}.

    After:
      The {{c2::memory}} is one  of the main components of the Von Neumann architecture; it
stores {{c3::data}} as well as the {{c4::program}} to be {{c5::executed}}. In an {{c6::imperative language}}, the part
of the {{c2::memory}} used to store data is {{c7::abstracted}} by {{c1::variables}}, which represent {{c2::memory}} cells. {{c6::Imperative languages}} provide {{c9::assignment}} statements to modify their contents.

============================================================

Note ID: 1585653945963
  Field: Text
    Before:
      In languages with {{c1::dynamic typing}}, such as Python, PHP and
JavaScript, variables are given {{c3::types}} at {{c2::run time}}, when {{c4::values}} are {{c4::assigned}} to them.
This provides some {{c5::flexibility,}} but makes it impossible to {{c6::type check}} programs at
{{c7::compile time}} and for this reason {{c8::less}} {{c9::errors}} can be detected before the programs
are {{c10::executed}}. Moreover, code added to perform the {{c6::type checking}} at run time in
{{c1::dynamically typed}} languages introduces {{c11::overheads}}.

    After:
      In languages with {{c1::dynamic typing}}, such as Python, PHP and
JavaScript, variables are given {{c3::types}} at {{c2::run time}}, when {{c4::values}} are {{c4::assigned}} to them.
This provides some {{c5::flexibility,}} but makes it impossible to {{c6::type check}} programs at
{{c7::compile time}} and for this reason {{c8::less}} {{c9::errors}} can be detected before the programs
are executed. Moreover, code added to perform the {{c6::type checking}} at run time in
{{c1::dynamically typed}} languages introduces overheads.

============================================================

Note ID: 1585657073520
  Field: Text
    Before:
      The {{c1::garbage collector}} is the part of the {{c2::run time}} {{c3::support}} that performs {{c4::deallocation}}
of {{c5::unused segments}} of the heap, returning them to the {{c6::pool}} of {{c7::available}} space.
In a language with implicit {{c4::deallocation}} of heap variables, the {{c2::run time}} {{c3::support}}
must include mechanisms to detect if a variable in the heap is no longer needed.
This is usually done by keeping {{c8::count}} of the number of {{c9::pointers}} to each variable
in the heap, with the associated {{c2::run time}} {{c10::overheads}}.&nbsp;

    After:
      The {{c1::garbage collector}} is the part of the {{c2::run time}} {{c3::support}} that performs {{c4::deallocation}}
of {{c5::unused segments}} of the heap, returning them to the {{c6::pool}} of {{c7::available}} space.
In a language with implicit {{c4::deallocation}} of heap variables, the {{c2::run time}} {{c3::support}}
must include mechanisms to detect if a variable in the heap is no longer needed.
This is usually done by keeping {{c8::count}} of the number of {{c9::pointers}} to each variable
in the heap, with the associated {{c2::run time}} overheads.&nbsp;

============================================================

Note ID: 1687532761840
  Field: Text
    Before:
      For the MSE&nbsp;\(l(\vec{w}; (x,y)) = (y-f_{\vec{w}}(x))^2\), on the dataset \(D_{tr}\)we can decompose the error of a model&nbsp;\(\vec{w}\) in the solution space&nbsp;\(W_{D_{tr}}\) when tested on dataset&nbsp;\(D_{te}\) as follows:<br><ul><li>{{c4::\(E_{\vec{w} \in W_{D_{tr} } }&nbsp; \mathrm{Err}_{D_{te} } (\vec{w}) \)}} =&nbsp;{{c1::\(Bias\)}} + {{c1::\(Var\)}}<br></li><li>{{c1::\(Bias\)}} = \(E_{(x,y) \in D_{te} }\)&nbsp;{{c2:: \( (y-\bar f_{D_{tr} }(x) )^2 \) }}</li><li>{{c1::\(Var\)}} = \(E_{(x,y) \in D_{te} }\) {{c3::\( E_{\vec{w} \in W_{D_tr} }\)&nbsp;\([&nbsp; (f_{\vec{w} }(x) - \bar f_{D_{tr}(x) })^2&nbsp; ]\)}}<br></li><li>Where {{c5::\( \bar f_{D_{tr} } = E_{\vec{w} \in W_{D_{tr} } }[f_w(\cdot)] \)}} is {{c6::the expected output of all models in the ensemble}}<br></li><li>The {{c1::bias}} denotes the {{c1::bias}} between the ground truth y and the ensembe output&nbsp;\(\bar f_{D_{tr}}\)</li><li>The {{c1::variance}} denotes {{c10::{{c7::the expected mse}}}} between {{c9::{{c7::&nbsp;\(f_{\vec{w} }(x)\) and&nbsp;\(\bar f_{D_{tr} }(x)\)}}}} which depends on the discrepancy between&nbsp;{{c8::{{c7::\(D_{tr}\) and&nbsp;\(D_{te}\)}}}}</li></ul>

    After:
      For the MSE&nbsp;\(l(\vec{w}; (x,y)) = (y-f_{\vec{w}}(x))^2\), on the dataset \(D_{tr}\)we can decompose the error of a model&nbsp;\(\vec{w}\) in the solution space&nbsp;\(W_{D_{tr}}\) when tested on dataset&nbsp;\(D_{te}\) as follows:<br><ul><li>{{c4::\(E_{\vec{w} \in W_{D_{tr} } }&nbsp; \mathrm{Err}_{D_{te} } (\vec{w}) \)}} =&nbsp;{{c1::\(Bias\)}} + {{c1::\(Var\)}}<br></li><li>{{c1::\(Bias\)}} = \(E_{(x,y) \in D_{te} }\)&nbsp;{{c2:: \( (y-\bar f_{D_{tr} }(x) )^2 \) }}</li><li>{{c1::\(Var\)}} = \(E_{(x,y) \in D_{te} }\) {{c3::\( E_{\vec{w} \in W_{D_tr} }\)&nbsp;\([&nbsp; (f_{\vec{w} }(x) - \bar f_{D_{tr}(x) })^2&nbsp; ]\)}}<br></li><li>Where {{c5::\( \bar f_{D_{tr} } = E_{\vec{w} \in W_{D_{tr} } }[f_w(\cdot)] \)}} is {{c6::the expected output of all models in the ensemble}}<br></li><li>The {{c1::bias}} denotes the {{c1::bias}} between the ground truth y and the ensembe output&nbsp;\(\bar f_{D_{tr}}\)</li><li>The {{c1::variance}} denotes {{c7::the expected mse}} between {{c9::{{c7::&nbsp;\(f_{\vec{w} }(x)\) and&nbsp;\(\bar f_{D_{tr} }(x)\)}}}} which depends on the discrepancy between&nbsp;{{c8::{{c7::\(D_{tr}\) and&nbsp;\(D_{te}\)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

Note ID: 1687799494377
  Field: Text
    Before:
      The uniform global model trained using FL&nbsp;\(h_{\hat{\mu}}\) , with probability at least&nbsp;\(1-\delta\) the minizer of empirical risk on the uniform distribution satistiefs the following bound on the data of client 1 with dataset size m:<br><ul><li>{{c11::{{c10::{{c9::{{c8::{{c1::\(L_{D_1}(h_\hat{\mu})&nbsp; - L_{D_1}( h_{D_1}) \)}}}}}}}}}} =&nbsp;\(\mathcal{O}(\) {{c11::{{c10::{{c9::{{c8::{{c2:: \(\sqrt{d + \log(\tfrac{1}{\delta} })&nbsp; \)}}}}}}}}}} {{c11::{{c10::{{c9::{{c3::\( / \sqrt{m} )\)}}}}}}}} +{{c11::{{c10::{{c4::&nbsp;\(max_{h \in H}\lvert L_{D_1}(h) - L_{D_2}(h) \rvert\)}}}}}}<br></li><li>{{c11::{{c7::{{c5::\(\hat{\mu} \)}} = {{c6::\( \sum_{c \ in C} p_c D_c\)}}}}}}<br></li><li>Since in most situations&nbsp;{{c12::\(m\) is small}} the global model usually performs {{c13::better}}</li></ul>

    After:
      The uniform global model trained using FL&nbsp;\(h_{\hat{\mu}}\) , with probability at least&nbsp;\(1-\delta\) the minizer of empirical risk on the uniform distribution satistiefs the following bound on the data of client 1 with dataset size m:<br><ul><li>{{c9::{{c8::{{c1::\(L_{D_1}(h_\hat{\mu})&nbsp; - L_{D_1}( h_{D_1}) \)}}}}}} =&nbsp;\(\mathcal{O}(\) {{c9::{{c8::{{c2:: \(\sqrt{d + \log(\tfrac{1}{\delta} })&nbsp; \)}}}}}} {{c9::{{c3::\( / \sqrt{m} )\)}}}} +{{c4::&nbsp;\(max_{h \in H}\lvert L_{D_1}(h) - L_{D_2}(h) \rvert\)}}<br></li><li>{{c7::{{c5::\(\hat{\mu} \)}} = {{c6::\( \sum_{c \ in C} p_c D_c\)}}}}<br></li><li>Since in most situations&nbsp;\(m\) is small the global model usually performs better</li></ul>

Note ID: 1687906283671
  Field: Text
    Before:
      To set the reward given by the {{c1::explored}} cohort for its exploration-exploitation algorithm, Auxo:<br><ul><li>Uses the {{c2::l2 norm}} of {{c2::the difference between the client update and the average cohort gradient}} as the {{c3::divergence&nbsp;\(D_c\)}}</li><li>Quantifies the instant reward as:</li><ul><li>&nbsp;\(\Delta R\) ={{c6::{{c4::&nbsp;\(1 \)}}}} - {{c6::{{c5::\( \frac{D_c}{avg(D) + b \cdot std(D)} \)}}}}</li><li>With a usual {{c6::{{c5::b = {{c7::1}}}}}}</li></ul></ul><ul><li>A client with a {{c10::{{c8::negative}}}}&nbsp;\(\Delta R\) is considered an {{c10::{{c9::outlier}}}}</li><li>Auxo updates the reward between each clients and its {{c1::explored}} cohort with a {{c11::discounting factor}} \(\gamma\) as</li><ul><li>\(R\) ={{c14::{{c12::&nbsp;\(\gamma \Delta R\)}}}} + {{c14::{{c13::\( (1-\gamma) R \)}}}}<br></li></ul></ul><br>

    After:
      To set the reward given by the {{c1::explored}} cohort for its exploration-exploitation algorithm, Auxo:<br><ul><li>Uses the {{c2::l2 norm}} of {{c2::the difference between the client update and the average cohort gradient}} as the {{c3::divergence&nbsp;\(D_c\)}}</li><li>Quantifies the instant reward as:</li><ul><li>&nbsp;\(\Delta R\) ={{c6::{{c4::&nbsp;\(1 \)}}}} - {{c6::{{c5::\( \frac{D_c}{avg(D) + b \cdot std(D)} \)}}}}</li><li>With a usual {{c6::{{c5::b = {{c7::1}}}}}}</li></ul></ul><ul><li>A client with a {{c8::negative}}&nbsp;\(\Delta R\) is considered an {{c9::outlier}}</li><li>Auxo updates the reward between each clients and its {{c1::explored}} cohort with a discounting factor \(\gamma\) as</li><ul><li>\(R\) =&nbsp;\(\gamma \Delta R\) + \( (1-\gamma) R \)<br></li></ul></ul><br>

    Before:
      <div>Example 3.7.4. Continuing as in the previous example with a particle taking a random walk on the number line with steps of size 1, let \(D\) be the particle's distance from the origin after \(n\) steps having estabished that the functions position is \(Y\) = \(2 \mathrm{Bin}(n,1/2) -n\). Assume that \(n\) is even. Find the PMF of \(D\).</div>
<div><br></div>
<div>Solution:</div>
<ul>
<li>We can write \(D\) = \(|Y|\); this is a function of \(Y\), but it isn't one-to-one as D=0 is the same as the event Y=0</li>
<li>For k even, D = k is the&nbsp; same as the event {{c4::{{c9::{{c10::{{c12::{{c13::\(\{Y=k\}\)}}}}}}}}}} {{c6::{{c13::\(\cup\)}}}} {{c5::{{c10::{{c13::\(\{Y=-k\}\)}}}}}}. So the PMF of \(D\) is</li>
<li>\(P\)&nbsp; (\(D=0\)) =&nbsp;{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c13::\(\binom{n}{\frac{n}{2}}}}}}}}}}}}\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\((\frac{1}{2})^n\)</li>
<li>\(P\)&nbsp; (\(D=k\)) =&nbsp;</li>
<ul>
<li>&nbsp;= \(P\) {{c5::{{c10::{{c13::(\(Y=k\))}}}}}} + \(P\){{c4::{{c9::{{c10::{{c12::{{c13::(\(Y=-k\))}}}}}}}}}}</li>
<li>={{c3::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::&nbsp;\(2\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c1::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c13::\(\binom{n}{\frac{n}{2} }}}}}}}}}}}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c13::&nbsp;\((\frac{1}{2})^n\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<div> •  • for \(k=2,4, \ldots, n\). In the final step we used symmetry (imagine a new random walk that moves left each time our random walk moves right, and vice versa) to see that \(P(Y=k)=P(Y=-k)\).</div>

    After:
      <div>Example 3.7.4. Continuing as in the previous example with a particle taking a random walk on the number line with steps of size 1, let \(D\) be the particle's distance from the origin after \(n\) steps having estabished that the functions position is \(Y\) = \(2 \mathrm{Bin}(n,1/2) -n\). Assume that \(n\) is even. Find the PMF of \(D\).</div>
<div><br></div>
<div>Solution:</div>
<ul>
<li>We can write \(D\) = \(|Y|\); this is a function of \(Y\), but it isn't one-to-one as D=0 is the same as the event Y=0</li>
<li>For k even, D = k is the&nbsp; same as the event {{c4::{{c9::\(\{Y=k\}\)}}}} {{c6::\(\cup\)}} {{c5::\(\{Y=-k\}\)}}. So the PMF of \(D\) is</li>
<li>\(P\)&nbsp; (\(D=0\)) =&nbsp;{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::\(\binom{n}{\frac{n}{2}}}}}}}}\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\((\frac{1}{2})^n\)</li>
<li>\(P\)&nbsp; (\(D=k\)) =&nbsp;</li>
<ul>
<li>&nbsp;= \(P\) {{c5::(\(Y=k\))}} + \(P\){{c4::{{c9::(\(Y=-k\))}}}}</li>
<li>={{c3::{{c8::{{c9::&nbsp;\(2\)}}}}}} {{c1::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::\(\binom{n}{\frac{n}{2} }}}}}}}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::&nbsp;\((\frac{1}{2})^n\)}}}}}}}}</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<div> •  • for \(k=2,4, \ldots, n\). In the final step we used symmetry (imagine a new random walk that moves left each time our random walk moves right, and vice versa) to see that \(P(Y=k)=P(Y=-k)\).</div>

      Definition 3.7.5 (Function of two r.v.s). Given an experiment with sample space \(S\), if \(X\) and \(Y\) are r.v.s that map \(s \in S\) to \(X(s)\) and \(Y(s)\) respectively, then \(g(X, Y)\) is the r.v. that maps \(s\) to \(g(X(s), Y(s))\).<br><br>Note that we are assuming that \(X\) and \(Y\) are defined on the {{c1::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c11::same sample space \(S\)}}}}}}}}}}.&nbsp;Usually we assume that \(S\) is chosen to be rich enough to encompass {{c2::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c11::whatever r.v.s we wish to work with}}}}}}}}}}}}. For example, if \(X\) is based on a coin flip and \(Y\) is based on a die roll, and we initially were using the sample space \(S_{1}=\{H, T\}\) for \(X\) and the sample space \(S_{2}=\{1,2,3,4,5,6\}\) for \(Y\), we can easily redefine \(X\) and \(Y\) so that both are defined on the richer space \(S\) = {{c5::{{c11::\(S_{1}\)}}}} {{c3::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::\(\times\)}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c5::{{c11::\( S_{2}\)}}}} = {{c4::{{c8::{{c10::{{c11::\(\left\{\left(s_{1}, s_{2}\right): s_{1} \in S_{1}, s_{2} \in S_{2}\right\}\).}}}}}}}}

      3.7.7 ({{c1::{{c8::{{c9::Category}}}}}} errors and {{c2::{{c8::{{c9::sympathetic}}}}}} magic). Many common mistakes in probability can be traced to confusing two of the following fundamental objects with each other: distributions, random variables, events, and numbers. Such mistakes are examples of {{c1::{{c8::{{c8::{{c8::{{c9::{{c9::{{c9::category}}}}}}}}}}}}}} errors. In general, a {{c1::{{c8::{{c8::{{c8::{{c9::{{c9::{{c9::category}}}}}}}}}}}}}} error is a mistake that doesn't just {{c3::{{c9::happen to be wrong}}}}, but in fact is {{c4::necessarily}} wrong since it is based on the wrong {{c1::{{c8::{{c8::{{c8::{{c9::{{c9::{{c9::category}}}}}}}}}}}}}} of object. To help avoid being {{c1::{{c8::{{c9::categorically}}}}}} wrong, always think about what {{c1::{{c8::{{c8::{{c8::{{c9::{{c9::{{c9::category}}}}}}}}}}}}}} an answer should have.<br><br>An especially common {{c1::{{c8::{{c8::{{c8::{{c9::{{c9::{{c9::category}}}}}}}}}}}}}} error is to confuse a random variable with {{c5::its distribution}}. We call this error {{c2::{{c8::{{c9::sympathetic}}}}}} magic; this term comes from anthropology, where it is used for the belief that one can {{c7::influence an object}} by manipulating a {{c6::representation of that object}}.&nbsp;

      Example 3.8.12 (Matching pennies). Consider the simple game called matching pennies. Each of two players, A and B, has a fair penny. They flip their pennies independently. So \(X\) and \(Y\) are {{c5::{{c11::conditionally dependent}}}} given \(Z\).

      Example 3.8.14 (Mystery opponent). Suppose that you are going to play two games of tennis against one of two identical twins. Against one of the twins, you are evenly matched, and against the other you have a 3/4 chance of winning. Suppose that you can't tell which twin you are playing against until after the two games. Let \(Z\) be the indicator of playing against the twin with whom you're evenly matched, and let \(X\) and \(Y\) be the indicators of victory in the first and second games, respectively.<br><br>Conditional on \(Z=1, X\) and \(Y\) are {{c2::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c11::i.i.d.}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c1::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c11::Bern(1/2)}}}}}}}}}}, and conditional on \(Z=0\), \(X\) and \(Y\) are {{c2::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c11::i.i.d.}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c3::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::Bern(3/4)}}}}}}}}}}}}. So \(X\) and \(Y\) are {{c4::{{c8::{{c10::{{c11::conditionally independent}}}}}}}}&nbsp; given \(Z\). Unconditionally, \(X\) and \(Y\) are {{c5::{{c11::dependent}}}} because observing \(X=1\) makes it more likely that we are playing the twin who is worse. That is,<br><br>{{c5::{{c11::\[<br>P(Y=1 \mid X=1)&gt;P(Y=1)<br>\]<br>}}}}<br>Past games give us information which helps us infer who our opponent is, which in turn helps us predict future games! Note that this example is isomorphic to the "random coin" scenario from Example 2.3.7.

    After:
      Example 3.8.14 (Mystery opponent). Suppose that you are going to play two games of tennis against one of two identical twins. Against one of the twins, you are evenly matched, and against the other you have a 3/4 chance of winning. Suppose that you can't tell which twin you are playing against until after the two games. Let \(Z\) be the indicator of playing against the twin with whom you're evenly matched, and let \(X\) and \(Y\) be the indicators of victory in the first and second games, respectively.<br><br>Conditional on \(Z=1, X\) and \(Y\) are {{c2::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::i.i.d.}}}}}}}}}} {{c1::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::Bern(1/2)}}}}}}}}, and conditional on \(Z=0\), \(X\) and \(Y\) are {{c2::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::i.i.d.}}}}}}}}}} {{c3::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::Bern(3/4)}}}}}}}}. So \(X\) and \(Y\) are {{c4::{{c8::conditionally independent}}}}&nbsp; given \(Z\). Unconditionally, \(X\) and \(Y\) are {{c5::dependent}} because observing \(X=1\) makes it more likely that we are playing the twin who is worse. That is,<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>P(Y=1 \mid X=1)&gt;P(Y=1)<br>\]<br>}}<br>Past games give us information which helps us infer who our opponent is, which in turn helps us predict future games! Note that this example is isomorphic to the "random coin" scenario from Example 2.3.7.

      <div>Example 3.9.1 (Fisher exact test). A scientist wishes to study whether women or men are more likely to have a certain disease, or whether they are equally likely. A random sample of \(n\) women and \(m\) men is gathered, and each person is tested for the disease (assume for this problem that the test is completely accurate). The numbers of women and men in the sample who have the disease are \(X\) and \(Y\) respectively, with \(X \sim \operatorname{Bin}\left(n, p_{1}\right)\) and \(Y \sim \operatorname{Bin}\left(m, p_{2}\right)\), independently. Here \(p_{1}\) and \(p_{2}\) are unknown, and we are interested in testing whether \(p_{1}=p_{2}\) (this is known as a null hypothesis in statistics).</div><br>Consider a \(2 \times 2\) table with rows corresponding to disease status and columns corresponding to gender. Each entry is the count of how many people have that disease status and gender, so \(n+m\) is the sum of all 4 entries. Suppose that it is observed that \(X+Y=r\).<br><br>The Fisher exact test is based on conditioning on both the row and column sums, so \(n, m, r\) are all treated as fixed, and then seeing if the observed value of \(X\) is "extreme" compared to this conditional distribution. Assuming the null hypothesis, find the conditional PMF of \(X\) given \(X+Y=r\).<div>Solution:</div><div><ul><li>{{c7::{{c14::{{c17::<img src="paste-e6dd12269971751ce826eb9e2ba445450f0b344f.jpg">::table}}::table}}::table}}<br></li><li>Next, let's compute the conditional PMF \(P(X=x \mid X+Y=r)\). By Bayes' rule,<br></li><li>\(P\) \((X=x \mid X+Y=r)\)&nbsp;</li><li>= {{c1::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(\frac{P(X+Y=r \mid X=x) P(X=x)}{P(X+Y=r)} \)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>= {{c2::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c17::\(\frac{P(Y=r-x) P(X=x)}{P(X+Y=r)} \)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}<br></li><li>The step {{c2::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c17::\(P(X+Y=r \mid X=x)=P(Y=r-x)\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} is justified by the independence of \(X\) and \(Y\). Assuming the {{c3::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::null hypothesis}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} and letting {{c3::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::\(p\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} = {{c3::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::\(p_{1}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} = {{c3::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::\( p_{2}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}, we have \(X \sim \operatorname{Bin}(n, p)\) and \(Y \sim \operatorname{Bin}(m, p)\), independently, so \(X+Y \sim\)&nbsp; {{c4::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c16::{{c17::\(\operatorname{Bin}(n+m, p)\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}. Thus,<br></li><li>\(P(X=x \mid X+Y=r)\)&nbsp;</li><li>= {{c6::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(\frac{\left(\begin{array}{c}m \\r-x\end{array}\right) p^{r-x}(1-p)^{mr+x} \left(\begin{array}{c}n \\x\end{array}\right) p^{x}(1-p)^{n-x} }{\left(\begin{array}{c}n+m \\r\end{array}\right) p^{r}(1-p)^{n+m-r} } \)}}}}}}}}</li><li>= {{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(\frac{\left(\begin{array}{c}n \\x\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{c}m \\r-x\end{array}\right)}{\left(\begin{array}{c}n+m \\r\end{array}\right)} \)}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>So the conditional distribution of \(X\) is {{c8::{{c17::Hypergeometric}}}} with parameters \(n, m, r\).<br></li></ul></div>

    After:
      <div>Example 3.9.1 (Fisher exact test). A scientist wishes to study whether women or men are more likely to have a certain disease, or whether they are equally likely. A random sample of \(n\) women and \(m\) men is gathered, and each person is tested for the disease (assume for this problem that the test is completely accurate). The numbers of women and men in the sample who have the disease are \(X\) and \(Y\) respectively, with \(X \sim \operatorname{Bin}\left(n, p_{1}\right)\) and \(Y \sim \operatorname{Bin}\left(m, p_{2}\right)\), independently. Here \(p_{1}\) and \(p_{2}\) are unknown, and we are interested in testing whether \(p_{1}=p_{2}\) (this is known as a null hypothesis in statistics).</div><br>Consider a \(2 \times 2\) table with rows corresponding to disease status and columns corresponding to gender. Each entry is the count of how many people have that disease status and gender, so \(n+m\) is the sum of all 4 entries. Suppose that it is observed that \(X+Y=r\).<br><br>The Fisher exact test is based on conditioning on both the row and column sums, so \(n, m, r\) are all treated as fixed, and then seeing if the observed value of \(X\) is "extreme" compared to this conditional distribution. Assuming the null hypothesis, find the conditional PMF of \(X\) given \(X+Y=r\).<div>Solution:</div><div><ul><li>{{c7::<img src="paste-e6dd12269971751ce826eb9e2ba445450f0b344f.jpg">::table::table::table}}<br></li><li>Next, let's compute the conditional PMF \(P(X=x \mid X+Y=r)\). By Bayes' rule,<br></li><li>\(P\) \((X=x \mid X+Y=r)\)&nbsp;</li><li>= {{c1::{{c9::\(\frac{P(X+Y=r \mid X=x) P(X=x)}{P(X+Y=r)} \)}}}}</li><li>= {{c2::{{c9::\(\frac{P(Y=r-x) P(X=x)}{P(X+Y=r)} \)}}}}<br></li><li>The step {{c2::{{c9::\(P(X+Y=r \mid X=x)=P(Y=r-x)\)}}}} is justified by the independence of \(X\) and \(Y\). Assuming the {{c3::null hypothesis}} and letting {{c3::\(p\)}} = {{c3::\(p_{1}\)}} = {{c3::\( p_{2}\)}}, we have \(X \sim \operatorname{Bin}(n, p)\) and \(Y \sim \operatorname{Bin}(m, p)\), independently, so \(X+Y \sim\)&nbsp; {{c4::\(\operatorname{Bin}(n+m, p)\)}}. Thus,<br></li><li>\(P(X=x \mid X+Y=r)\)&nbsp;</li><li>= {{c6::\(\frac{\left(\begin{array}{c}m \\r-x\end{array}\right) p^{r-x}(1-p)^{mr+x} \left(\begin{array}{c}n \\x\end{array}\right) p^{x}(1-p)^{n-x} }{\left(\begin{array}{c}n+m \\r\end{array}\right) p^{r}(1-p)^{n+m-r} } \)}}</li><li>= {{c5::\(\frac{\left(\begin{array}{c}n \\x\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{c}m \\r-x\end{array}\right)}{\left(\begin{array}{c}n+m \\r\end{array}\right)} \)}}</li><li>So the conditional distribution of \(X\) is {{c8::Hypergeometric}} with parameters \(n, m, r\).<br></li></ul></div>

    Before:
      <div>Example 3.9.1 (Fisher exact test). A scientist wishes to study whether women or men are more likely to have a certain disease, or whether they are equally likely. A random sample of \(n\) women and \(m\) men is gathered, and each person is tested for the disease (assume for this problem that the test is completely accurate). The numbers of women and men in the sample who have the disease are \(X\) and \(Y\) respectively, with \(X \sim \operatorname{Bin}\left(n, p_{1}\right)\) and \(Y \sim \operatorname{Bin}\left(m, p_{2}\right)\), independently. Here \(p_{1}\) and \(p_{2}\) are unknown, and we are interested in testing whether \(p_{1}=p_{2}\) (this is known as a null hypothesis in statistics).</div><br>Consider a \(2 \times 2\) table with rows corresponding to disease status and columns corresponding to gender. Each entry is the count of how many people have that disease status and gender, so \(n+m\) is the sum of all 4 entries. Suppose that it is observed that \(X+Y=r\).<br><br>The Fisher exact test is based on conditioning on both the row and column sums, so \(n, m, r\) are all treated as fixed, and then seeing if the observed value of \(X\) is "extreme" compared to this conditional distribution. Assuming the null hypothesis, find the conditional PMF of \(X\) given \(X+Y=r\).<div>Solution:</div><div><ul><li>First we'll build the \(2 \times 2\) table, treating \(n\), \(m\), and \(r\) as fixed.<br></li><li><img src="paste-f1710341c3dfcae65201fdbf2de98556c3c43db5.jpg"><br></li></ul><div>Next, let's compute the conditional PMF \(P(X=x \mid X+Y=r)\). By Bayes' rule,<br><br>\[<br>\begin{aligned}<br>P(X=x \mid X+Y=r) &amp; =\frac{P(X+Y=r \mid X=x) P(X=x)}{P(X+Y=r)} \\<br>&amp; =\frac{P(Y=r-x) P(X=x)}{P(X+Y=r)} .<br>\end{aligned}<br>\]<br><br>The step \(P(X+Y=r \mid X=x)=P(Y=r-x)\) is justified by the independence of \(X\) and \(Y\). Assuming the null hypothesis and letting \(p=p_{1}=p_{2}\), we have \(X \sim \operatorname{Bin}(n, p)\) and \(Y \sim \operatorname{Bin}(m, p)\), independently, so \(X+Y \sim \operatorname{Bin}(n+m, p)\). Thus,<br><br>\[<br>\begin{aligned}<br>P(X=x \mid X+Y=r) &amp; =\frac{\left(\begin{array}{c}<br>m \\<br>r-x<br>\end{array}\right) p^{r-x}(1-p)^{m-r+x}\left(\begin{array}{c}<br>n \\<br>x<br>\end{array}\right) p^{x}(1-p)^{n-x}}{\left(\begin{array}{c}<br>n+m \\<br>r<br>\end{array}\right) p^{r}(1-p)^{n+m-r}} \\<br>&amp; =\frac{\left(\begin{array}{c}<br>n \\<br>x<br>\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{c}<br>m \\<br>r-x<br>\end{array}\right)}{\left(\begin{array}{c}<br>n+m \\<br>r<br>\end{array}\right)}<br>\end{aligned}<br>\]<br><br>So the conditional distribution of \(X\) is Hypergeometric with parameters \(n, m, r\).<br></div></div><div><br></div><div>To understand why the Hypergeometric appeared, seemingly out of nowhere, let's connect this problem to the elk story for the Hypergeometric. In the elk story, we are interested in the distribution of the number of tagged elk in the recaptured sample. By analogy, think of women as tagged elk and men as untagged elk. Instead of recapturing \(r\) elk at random from the forest, we infect {{cw::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c11::\(X+Y\)}}}}}}}}}}}} ={{c1::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c11::\(r\)}}}}}}}}}} people with the disease; under the {{c3::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::null hypothesis}}}}}}}}}}}}, the set of diseased people is {{c3::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::equally likely}}}}}}}}}}}} to be {{c4::{{c8::{{c10::{{c11::any set of \(r\) people}}}}}}}}. Thus, conditional on {{c2::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c11::\(X+Y\)}}}}}}}}}}}} = {{c1::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c11::\(r, X\)}}}}}}}}}} represents the number of women among the \(r\) diseased individuals. This is exactly analogous to the number of tagged elk in the recaptured sample, which is distributed {{c5::{{c11::\(\operatorname{HGeom}(n, m, r)\).}}}}</div>

      An interesting fact, which turns out to be useful in statistics, is that the conditional distribution of \(X\) does not depend on \(p\) when considering null hypothesis tests : unconditionally, \(X \sim\) {{c4::{{c8::{{c10::{{c11::\( \operatorname{Bin}(n, p)\)}}}}}}}}, but \(p\) {{c5::{{c11::disappears from the parameters of the conditional distribution}}}}! This makes sense upon reflection, since once we know \(X+Y=r\), we can work directly with the fact that we have a population with {{c1::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c11::\(r\)}}}}}}}}}} diseased and {{c2::{{c6::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c11::\(n+m-r\)}}}}}}}}}}}} healthy people, without worrying about the value of \(p\) that {{c3::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::originally generated the population.}}}}}}}}}}}}

      Theorem 3.9.3. If \(X \sim \operatorname{HGeom}(w, b, n)\) and \(N=w+b \rightarrow \infty\) such that \(p=\) \(w /(w+b)\) remains fixed, then the PMF of \(X\) converges to the \(\operatorname{Bin}(n, p) \mathrm{PMF}\).<br><br>Proof. We take the stated limit of the \(\operatorname{HGeom}(w, b, n)\) PMF:<br><br><ul><li>\(P(X=k)\)&nbsp;</li><li>={{c1::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c13::\( \frac{\left(\begin{array}{l}w \\k\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{c}b \\n-k\end{array}\right)}{\left(\begin{array}{c}w+b \\n\end{array}\right)} \)}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>= {{c2::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c13::\(\left(\begin{array}{l}n \\k\end{array}\right)\frac{\left(\begin{array}{c}w+b-n \\w-k\end{array}\right)}{\left(\begin{array}{c}w+b \\w\end{array}\right)} \quad \text { by Theorem 3.4.5 } \)&nbsp;}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>= {{c3::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::\(\left(\begin{array}{l}n \\k\end{array}\right) \frac{w !}{(w-k) !} \frac{b !}{(b-n+k) !} \frac{(w+b-n) !}{(w+b) !} \)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>= {{c4::{{c9::{{c10::{{c12::{{c13::\(\left(\begin{array}{l}n \\k\end{array}\right) \frac{w(w-1) \ldots(w-k+1) b(b-1) \ldots(b-n+k+1)}{(w+b)(w+b-1) \ldots(w+b-n+1)} \)}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>= {{c5::{{c10::{{c13::\(\left(\begin{array}{l}n \\k\end{array}\right) \frac{p\left(p-\frac{1}{N}\right) \ldots\left(p-\frac{k-1}{N}\right) q\left(q-\frac{1}{N}\right) \ldots\left(q-\frac{n-k-1}{N}\right)}{\left(1-\frac{1}{N}\right)\left(1-\frac{2}{N}\right) \ldots\left(1-\frac{n-1}{N}\right)} \)}}}}}}</li></ul><div>As \(N \rightarrow \infty\), the denominator goes to {{c3::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::1}}}}}}}}}}}}}} , and the numerator goes to {{c2::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c13::\(p^{k}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c1::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c13::\(q^{n-k}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}. Thus<br></div><div><ul><li>\(P\) \((X=k)\) {{c6::{{c13::\(\rightarrow\)}}}} {{c3::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::\(\left(\begin{array}{l}n \\k\end{array}\right)\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}&nbsp;{{c2::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c13::\(p^{k}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c1::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c13::\(q^{n-k}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}<br></li></ul></div>

Knowing the probabilities of these events for all \(x\) gives us {{c12::{{c18::{{c19::{{c20::{{c21::{{c22::{{c23::{{c24::{{c25::{{c26::{{c27::{{c28::{{c30::{{c31::the CDF}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} and (in the {{c14::{{c20::{{c21::{{c22::{{c23::{{c24::{{c25::{{c26::{{c27::{{c28::{{c31::discrete}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} case) {{c13::{{c19::{{c20::{{c21::{{c22::{{c23::{{c24::{{c25::{{c26::{{c27::{{c28::{{c31::the PMF}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}, taking us {{c15::{{c21::{{c22::{{c23::{{c24::{{c25::{{c26::{{c27::{{c28::{{c31::full circle.}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}

    After:
      {{c1::<img src="paste-a2d09eef286105ccff045f9315e6414b962b0a89.jpg">}}<br>\section*{FIGURE 3.12}<br><br>Four fundamental objects in probability: {{c1::distributions}} (blueprints), {{c2::random variables}}, {{c3::events}}, and {{c4::numbers}}. From a {{c5::CDF \(F\)}} we can {{c6::generate an r.v. \(X\)}}. From \(X\), we can {{c7::generate many other r.v.s}} by {{c8::taking functions of \(X\).}} There are various events describing the {{c9::behavior of \(X\)}}. Most notably, for any constant \(x\) the events \(X \leq x\) and \(X=x\) are of interest. Knowing the probabilities of these events for all \(x\) gives us the CDF and (in the discrete case) the PMF, taking us full circle.

Note ID: 1722091236243
  Field: Text
    Before:
      2. Consider the following conversation from an episode of The Simpsons:&nbsp;<br><br><i>Lisa: Dad, I think he's an ivory dealer! His boots are ivory, his hat is ivory, and I'm pretty sure that check is ivory.<br>Homer: Lisa, a guy who's got lots of ivory is less likely to hurt Stampy than a guy whose ivory supplies are low.</i><br><br>Here Homer and Lisa are debating the question of whether or not the man (named Blackheart) is likely to hurt Stampy the Elephant if they sell Stampy to him. They clearly disagree about how to use their observations about Blackheart to learn about the probability (conditional on the evidence) that Blackheart will hurt Stampy.<br><br><ul><li>Let H be the event that the man will hurt Stampy, let L be the event that a man has lots of ivory, and let D be the event that the man is an ivory dealer.<br></li><li>(c) Assume it is true that someone who has a lot of a commodity will have less desire to acquire more of the commodity. Explain what is wrong with Homer's reasoning that the evidence about Blackheart makes it less likely that he will harm Stampy.<br></li><ul><li>Homer does not realize that observing that Blackheart has so much ivory makes it much {{c3::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::more likely}}}}}}}}}}}}}} that Blackheart {{c1::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c13::is an ivory dealer,}}}}}}}}}}}} which in turn makes it more likely that the man will hurt Stampy. (This is an example of Simpson's Paradox.) It may be true that, controlling for whether or not {{c1::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c13::Blackheart is a dealer}}}}}}}}}}}}, having {{c2::{{c7::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c13::high ivory supplies}}}}}}}}}}}}}} makes it {{c3::{{c8::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::less likely}}}}}}}}}}}}}} that he will harm Stampy: {{c5::{{c10::{{c13::\(P(H \mid L, D)&lt;P\left(H \mid L^c, D\right)\)}}}}}} and {{c6::{{c13::\(P\left(H \mid L, D^c\right)&lt;P\left(H \mid L^c, D^c\right)\).}}}} However, this does not imply that {{c4::{{c9::{{c10::{{c12::{{c13::\(P(H \mid L)&lt;P\left(H \mid L^c\right)\).}}}}}}}}}}<br></li></ul></ul>

      2. Consider the following conversation from an episode of The Simpsons:&nbsp;<br><br><i>Lisa: Dad, I think he's an ivory dealer!       1. A gambler repeatedly plays a game where in each round, he wins a dollar with probability \(1 / 3\) and loses a dollar with probability \(2 / 3\). His strategy is "quit when he is ahead by \(\$ 2\)," though some suspect he is a gambling addict anyway. Suppose that he starts with a million dollars. Show that the probability that he'll ever be ahead by \(\$ 2\) is less than \(1 / 4\).<br><ul><li>\(P(W)\) =&nbsp;{{c8::{{c16::{{c19::\(P\left(W \mid A_1\right)\)}}}}}} {{c9::{{c19::\(P\left(A_1\right)\)&nbsp;}}}} +&nbsp;{{c9::{{c19::\(P\left(W \mid A_1^c\right)\)}}}}&nbsp; {{c8::{{c16::{{c19::\(P\left(A_1^c\right)\)}}}}}}<br></li><li>Let \(a_i\) be the probability that the gambler achieves {{c3::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::{{c18::{{c19::a profit of \(\$ 2\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} before {{c4::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c18::{{c19::being ruined}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}, starting with a fortune of \(\$ i\).&nbsp;</li><li>For our setup,&nbsp;</li><ul><li>\(P(W)\) = \(a_i\),&nbsp;</li><li>\(P\left(W \mid A_1\right) \) = \(a_{i+1}\)&nbsp;</li><li>and&nbsp;</li><li>\(P\left(W \mid A_1^c\right)\) = \(a_{i-1}\).&nbsp;</li><li>Therefore,</li><ul><li>\(a_i\)= {{c1::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c19::\(a_{i+1} / 3\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}+{{c2::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::{{c19::\(2 a_{i-1} / 3\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>with boundary conditions {{c6::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c19::\(a_0\)}}}}}}}}}} = {{c7::{{c15::{{c16::{{c19::\(0\)}}}}}}}} and {{c7::{{c15::{{c16::{{c19::\(a_{i+2}\)}}}}}}}} = {{c6::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c19::\(1\)}}}}}}}}}}. We can then solve this difference equation for \(a_i\) (directly or using the result of the gambler's ruin problem):<br></li><li>\(a_i\) =&nbsp;{{c5::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c19::\(2^i-1\)}}}}}}}}}}}} \(/\)&nbsp;{{c5::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c19::\(2^{2+i}-1\)}}}}}}}}}}}}<br></li></ul><li>Which is always less than 1/4</li></ul></ul>

    After:
      1. A gambler repeatedly plays a game where in each round, he wins a dollar with probability \(1 / 3\) and loses a dollar with probability \(2 / 3\). His strategy is "quit when he is ahead by \(\$ 2\)," though some suspect he is a gambling addict anyway. Suppose that he starts with a million dollars. Show that the probability that he'll ever be ahead by \(\$ 2\) is less than \(1 / 4\).<br><ul><li>\(P(W)\) =&nbsp;{{c8::\(P\left(W \mid A_1\right)\)}} {{c9::\(P\left(A_1\right)\)&nbsp;}} +&nbsp;{{c9::\(P\left(W \mid A_1^c\right)\)}}&nbsp; {{c8::\(P\left(A_1^c\right)\)}}<br></li><li>Let \(a_i\) be the probability that the gambler achieves {{c3::a profit of \(\$ 2\)}} before {{c4::being ruined}}, starting with a fortune of \(\$ i\).&nbsp;</li><li>For our setup,&nbsp;</li><ul><li>\(P(W)\) = \(a_i\),&nbsp;</li><li>\(P\left(W \mid A_1\right) \) = \(a_{i+1}\)&nbsp;</li><li>and&nbsp;</li><li>\(P\left(W \mid A_1^c\right)\) = \(a_{i-1}\).&nbsp;</li><li>Therefore,</li><ul><li>\(a_i\)= {{c1::\(a_{i+1} / 3\)}}+{{c2::\(2 a_{i-1} / 3\)}}</li><li>with boundary conditions {{c6::\(a_0\)}} = {{c7::\(0\)}} and {{c7::\(a_{i+2}\)}} = {{c6::\(1\)}}. We can then solve this difference equation for \(a_i\) (directly or using the result of the gambler's ruin problem):<br></li><li>\(a_i\) =&nbsp;{{c5::\(2^i-1\)}} \(/\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(2^{2+i}-1\)}}<br></li></ul><li>Which is always less than 1/4</li></ul></ul>

      (a) In the World Series of baseball, two teams (call them \(A\) and \(B\) ) play a sequence of games against each other, and the first team to win four games wins the series. Let \(p\) be the probability that \(A\) wins an individual game, and assume that the games are independent. What is the probability that team \(A\) wins the series?<br><br><ul><li>\(P(\text { A wins })\) ={{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(P \text { (A winning in } 4 \text { games })\)}}}}}}}}}} +&nbsp;{{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(P \text { (A winning in } 5 \text { games) }\)}}}}}}}}}} +{{c5::{{c12::&nbsp;{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(P \text { (A wins in } 6 \text { games })\)}}}}}}}}}} +&nbsp;{{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(P \text { (A wins in } 6 \text { games })\)}}}}}}}}}}<br></li><ul><li>=&nbsp;{{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(p^4\)}}}}}}}}}}+{{c1::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(\binom{4}{3}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c2::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c17::\(p^4\) \(q\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}+{{c6::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(\binom{5}{3}\)}}}}}}}} {{c7::{{c14::{{c17::\(p^4\) \(q^2\)}}}}}}+{{c7::{{c14::{{c17::\(\binom{6}{3}\)}}}}}} {{c6::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(p^4\) \(q^3\)}}}}}}}}</li></ul><li>Because, for example:</li><li>\(P(\mathrm{~A} \text { wins in } 5)\) =&nbsp;{{c3::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::\(P \text { (A wins } 3 \text { out of first } 4 \text { ) }\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} * {{c4::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c16::{{c17::\(P(\text { A wins } 5 \text { th game } \mid \mathrm{A} \text { wins } 3 \text { out of first } 4)\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}<br></li><li>={{c1::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(\binom{4}{3}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}{{c2::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c17::\(p^3 q p\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>(This value can also be found from the PMF of a distribution known as the<br>{{c8::{{c17::Negative Binomial}}}}, which we will see later in the course.)<br></li></ul>

    After:
      (a) In the World Series of baseball, two teams (call them \(A\) and \(B\) ) play a sequence of games against each other, and the first team to win four games wins the series. Let \(p\) be the probability that \(A\) wins an individual game, and assume that the games are independent.       2. A sequence of \(n\) independent experiments is performed. Each experiment is a success with probability \(p\) and a failure with probability \(q=1-p\). Show that conditional on the number of successes, all possibilities for the list of outcomes of the experiment are equally likely (of course, we only consider lists of outcomes where the number of successes is consistent with the information being conditioned on).<br><br><ul><li>Let \(X_j\) be 1 if the \(j\) th experiment is a success and 0 otherwise, and let \(X=\) \(X_1+\cdots+X_n\) be the total number of successes. Then for any \(k\) and any&nbsp;\(a_1, \ldots, a_n \in\{0,1\} \text { with } a_1+\cdots+a_n=k\)<br></li><li>\(P\left(X_1=a_1, \ldots, X_n=a_n \mid X=k\right)\) =&nbsp;<br></li><ul><li>= {{c1::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(P\left(X_1=a_1, \ldots, X_n=a_n, X=k\right)\)&nbsp;}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}\(/\){{c2::{{c9::&nbsp;{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c17::\(P(X=k)\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}<br></li><li>=&nbsp;{{c6::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(P\left(X_1=a_1, \ldots, X_n=a_n\right)\)}}}}}}}}&nbsp;\(/\) {{c2::{{c9::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c17::\(P(X=k)\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>={{c3::{{c10::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::&nbsp;\(p^k q^{n-k}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}&nbsp;\(/\)&nbsp;{{c4::{{c11::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c16::{{c17::\(\binom{n}{k} p^k q^{n-k}\)&nbsp;}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>= {{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(\frac{1}{\binom{n}{k} }\)}}}}}}}}}}</li></ul><li>This does not depend on \(a_1, \ldots, a_n\). Thus, for \(n\) independent Bernoulli trials, given that there are exactly \(k\) successes, the {{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(\binom{n}{k}\)}}}}}}}}}} possible sequences consisting of {{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(k\)}}}}}}}}}} successes and {{c5::{{c12::{{c13::{{c14::{{c17::\(n-k\)}}}}}}}}}} failures are equally likely. Interestingly, the conditional probability above also {{c8::{{c17::does not depend on \(p\)}}}} (this leads to the notion of a {{c7::{{c14::{{c17::sufficient statistic}}}}}}, which is studied in Stat 111).<br></li></ul>

    After:
      2. A sequence of \(n\) independent experiments is performed. Each experiment is a success with probability \(p\) and a failure with probability \(q=1-p\). Show that conditional on the number of successes, all possibilities for the list of outcomes of the experiment are equally likely (of course, we only consider lists of outcomes where the number of successes is consistent with the information being conditioned on).<br><br><ul><li>Let \(X_j\) be 1 if the \(j\) th experiment is a success and 0 otherwise, and let \(X=\) \(X_1+\cdots+X_n\) be the total number of successes. Then for any \(k\) and any&nbsp;\(a_1, \ldots, a_n \in\{0,1\} \text { with } a_1+\cdots+a_n=k\)<br></li><li>\(P\left(X_1=a_1, \ldots, X_n=a_n \mid X=k\right)\) =&nbsp;<br></li><ul><li>= {{c1::{{c9::\(P\left(X_1=a_1, \ldots, X_n=a_n, X=k\right)\)&nbsp;}}}}\(/\){{c2::{{c9::&nbsp;\(P(X=k)\)}}}}<br></li><li>=&nbsp;{{c6::\(P\left(X_1=a_1, \ldots, X_n=a_n\right)\)}}&nbsp;\(/\) {{c2::{{c9::\(P(X=k)\)}}}}</li><li>={{c3::&nbsp;\(p^k q^{n-k}\)}}&nbsp;\(/\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(\binom{n}{k} p^k q^{n-k}\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>= {{c5::\(\frac{1}{\binom{n}{k} }\)}}</li></ul><li>This does not depend on \(a_1, \ldots, a_n\). Thus, for \(n\) independent Bernoulli trials, given that there are exactly \(k\) successes, the {{c5::\(\binom{n}{k}\)}} possible sequences consisting of {{c5::\(k\)}} successes and {{c5::\(n-k\)}} failures are equally likely. Interestingly, the conditional probability above also {{c8::does not depend on \(p\)}} (this leads to the notion of a {{c7::sufficient statistic}}, which is studied in Stat 111).<br></li></ul>

      Proof that compact sets are closed:<br><ul><li>Pictographically: {{c1::<img src="paste-864c826b4377fd119e0a7488b59bc23b51c3a0fb.jpg">}}<br></li><li>Let K be compact and consider&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(\not \in\)&nbsp;\(K\)</li><li>We want to show that&nbsp;\(p\) has a neighbourhood that does not intersect K which is the same as showing it is not a limit point of K i.e it is interior to&nbsp;\(K^c\)</li><li>Or it is the same as showing that the complement of K is open</li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(q\)&nbsp;\(in\)&nbsp;\(K\), let&nbsp;{{c2::\(V_q\)}} =&nbsp;{{c1::\(N_{r/2} (q)\)}} be a {{c2::ball around q}} and {{c1::\(U_p\)}} =&nbsp;{{c2::\(N_{r/2} (p)\)}}</li><ul><li>&nbsp;Where&nbsp;\(r\) =&nbsp;{{c3::\(d\)&nbsp;\( (p,q)\)}}</li></ul><li>Notice that the collection {{c2::\({V_q}\)}} is an {{c4::open cover}} of K</li><li>By {{c5::compactness}} of K there {{c7::exists a finite subcover}}&nbsp;{{c6::\(V_{q_{n} }\)}}</li><li>Which allows us to pick the equivalent finite set from the complement of K</li><li>Define&nbsp;\(W\) =&nbsp;{{c9::\(\cap_n\)}} {{c8::\(U_{q_{n} }\)}} which is open because it is a finite intersection of open sets</li><li>Specifically it is a ball whose radius is the minimum distance between&nbsp;\(p_i\) and&nbsp;\(q_i\)</li><li>Pictographically: {{c3::<img src="paste-40a5b413ea121ae49567ef8eefa6c56396c2bf5e.jpg">}}</li><li>\(W\) \(\cap\) {{c6::\(V_{q_i}\)}} =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\) because&nbsp;\(W\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(U_{q_i}\) and&nbsp; \(U_{q_i}\) &nbsp;\(\cap\){{c6::\(V_{q_i}\)}} =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li><li>Thus W is the desired neighbourhood showing that any arbitrary point p is interrior&nbsp;to&nbsp;\(K^c\) meaning that&nbsp; \(K^c\) is open, making&nbsp;\(K\) closed</li></ul>

meaning that&nbsp; {{c1::{{c13::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::{{c18::{{c19::{{c20::{{c21::{{c22::{{c25::\(O_{\alpha^\prime}\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c4::{{c18::{{c19::{{c20::{{c21::{{c22::{{c25::covers}}}}}}}}}}}}}} {{c3::{{c17::{{c18::{{c19::{{c20::{{c21::{{c22::{{c25::\(I_m\)}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li><li>Which contradicts \(I_m\) {{c11::{{c14::{{c15::{{c16::{{c17::{{c18::{{c19::{{c20::{{c21::{{c22::{{c23::{{c24::{{c25::not having a finite subcover}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}}</li></ul>

      Proof that any closed interval [a,b] in R is compact:<br><ul><li>Assume the interval is not compact</li><li>Then there exists an open cover \(O_{\alpha}\) without a finite subcover&nbsp;</li><li>Pictographically: {{c6::<img src="paste-f454a7e35dc3ea3bcc0fdc1e9066cf9636efb0ae.jpg">}}</li><li>Divide the interval into&nbsp;\([a, c_1]\) and&nbsp;&nbsp;\([c_1, b]\)</li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha}\) covers&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;\([a, c_1]\)&nbsp; and \([c_1, b]\)</li><li>At least one of these intervals must {{c6::also not have a finite subcover}} otherwise you coult just {{c7::union two finite subcovers}}<br></li><li>Pick the one without a finite subcover&nbsp;\(I_1\) (WLOG \([a,c_1]\)</li><li>Then repeat the process splitting&nbsp;\(I_1\) into two halves by&nbsp;\(c_2\) to get&nbsp;\(I_2\)</li><li>Generally&nbsp;\(I_{n+1}\)&nbsp;{{c8::\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(I_n\)}} created by {{c9::difviding&nbsp;\(I_{n}\)}} by&nbsp;{{c9::\(c_n\)}} such that we choose the </li><li>half without a finite subcover</li><li></li><li>By the nested interval property there exists \(x\) such that {{c5::\(\cap_n I_n\)}} =&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;<br></li><li>Pictographically: {{c2::<img src="paste-dd9c9e556ab016c8ec5ac318d70901050a90a0c3.jpg">}}</li><li>\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha^\prime}\) for some&nbsp;\(\alpha^\prime\)<br></li><li>So there exists&nbsp;\(r\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\) such that&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(N_{r}(x)\)}}&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(\)&nbsp;{{c1::&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha^\prime}\)}}</li><li>Since the intervals get arbitrarily small, there {{c3::exists&nbsp;\(I_m\)&nbsp;}}\(\subset \)&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c2::\(N_{r}(x)\)}}&nbsp; meaning that&nbsp; {{c1::\(O_{\alpha^\prime}\)}} {{c4::covers}} {{c3::\(I_m\)}}</li><li>Which contradicts \(I_m\) not having a finite subcover</li></ul>

      \(R^n\) is complete proof:<br><ul><li>If&nbsp;\(x_n\) is Cauchy it is also {{c4::bounded}} because</li><ul><li>&nbsp;for&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) ={{c6::&nbsp;\(1\)}} there exists&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(d\)&nbsp;\((x_n,x_m)\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\forall n,m &gt; N\).&nbsp;</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(R\) =&nbsp;{{c9::\(\max\)}}&nbsp;{{c7::\((d(x_n,x_m),1)\)&nbsp;}}\(\forall\)&nbsp;{{c8::\(n,m\)}}</li><li>Then the sequence is {{c4::bounded}} by {{c1::&nbsp;\(N_{R}(x_n)\)}}</li></ul><li>Due to {{c4::boundedness}}, {{c1::\(N_{R}(x_n)\)}} is inside some {{c2::k-cell}} in&nbsp;\(R^n\), thus {{c1::the ball}} is {{c3::complete}}</li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(x_n\) {{c5::converges}} because the {{c1::k-cell}} is {{c3::complete}}</li></ul>
